        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135937333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3639174529263525267"><br/>2013<br/><br/>I think it must’ve been… I don’t know, maybe three or four years since I read <i>The Demonata</i> series? I probably wouldn’t have picked them up at all if it hadn’t been for my brother, because even though I <i>ogled</i> over <i>The Saga of Darren Shan</i> (a.k.a <i>The Cirque du Freak</i> series), the covers of these books alone was enough to throw me off, and I told myself I wouldn’t bet my money on <i>The Demonata</i>. Buying a book can sometimes be a gamble – how do you know it’s going to be one you’re going to want to r</span>
  <span id="freeText3639174529263525267" style="display:none"><br />2013<br /><br />I think it must’ve been… I don’t know, maybe three or four years since I read <i>The Demonata</i> series? I probably wouldn’t have picked them up at all if it hadn’t been for my brother, because even though I <i>ogled</i> over <i>The Saga of Darren Shan</i> (a.k.a <i>The Cirque du Freak</i> series), the covers of these books alone was enough to throw me off, and I told myself I wouldn’t bet my money on <i>The Demonata</i>. Buying a book can sometimes be a gamble – how do you know it’s going to be one you’re going to want to read again? I don’t think books are worth buying if you’re only going to read them once, or not even flip back to a certain chapter that stuck with you just to relive fond memories.<br />In any case, my brother’s friend had the first book of <i>Demonata, Lord Loss</i>, and I thought, what the hell, it’s accessible, it’s free, what have I got to lose. My brother fell in complete <i>love</i> with the book. Me, I thought it was okay, but a bit shoddy. It turned out the first book wasn’t really nearly as shoddy as the books following it.<br /><br />But…<br /><br />I’m giving this review of the series as a whole because, despite all the negative things I said about it while I was reading it, <i>The Demonata</i> has actually appeared more than once or twice in conversations I’ve had with my brother, and those conversations always consist of sentences like ‘Remember Dervish? And Kirilli? Remember Wolf Island and Bec? Shark? Timas and Beranabus?” And these little reminisces are punctuated with nostalgic smiles and laughs. And I got to thinking that, on the whole, this was a good story, and I actually <i>do</i> miss it.<br /><br />People who are planning to read this series don’t have to take my word for it, especially since<br /><br />a.	<b>I didn’t actually read all the books</b> – I skipped the second (<i>Demon Thief</i>), third (<i>Bec</i>, although my brother summarized it for me; he said it was the best one), some of the fifth (<i>Blood Beast</i>), and most of the sixth (<i>Demon Apocalypse</i>). The reasons were partly because these weren’t my books and my brother, who would read them first before handing them to me, was always anxious for me to finish quickly so he could trade the current for the next with his friend - and mostly because I wasn’t that in to the story at first, and I didn’t really believe I would read the whole series; just skimming through the chapters to get the main idea seemed fine to me. As I recall, in the end of one of the books there was a reversal of time back to the beginning of that book, so all the stuff that happened in between did not really happen (or it was as <i>if</i> it did not really happen). Perfectly okay to skip. But by the seventh book I was racing through every page and was actually beginning to enjoy them, I think maybe because of Bec, who is one of the few female characters of any story I actually liked and didn’t mind following in the first person POV, but also because things were picking up and the story turned out not so bad after all.<br /><br />b.	<b>I’m a girl.</b> I know that shouldn’t have much to do with it, but I guess this kind of series appeals more to guys than girls, and as I’ve said, my brother and his friends were more enthusiastic about <i>The Demonata</i> than I was. I couldn’t really get what made some scenes more awesome than others – I could only cringe at some of the more graphical paragraphs describing demons and humans being eaten/impaled/ripped apart. I could at least laugh at all the right parts. I think it really all narrows down to taste – on first sight this wouldn’t be the kind of series I would’ve chosen to read voluntarily.<br /><br />c.	<b>It’s been a long time since I’ve read the books</b>. My memories of the plots of each one are a bit hazy, and the only things that have stayed clear in my mind are certain bits of dialogue or scenes I particularly liked. I can’t even remember the names of most of the characters except for the most recurring (there were two demons who had the rather colorful names of Vein and Artery, and I even remember how these works of art looked like).<br /><br />d.	<b>I didn’t like the series at first, and really hated the ending</b>. I hadn’t even realized <i>how</i> much these books annoyed me until I read my old reviews for <i>Lord Loss</i> (below) and <i>Hell’s Heroes</i> (and those reviews sounded like the pouting of a ten-year old, although I think I must have actually been fourteen at least.) I guess there was just a lot of stuff I couldn’t take seriously, and maybe compared to <i>The Cirque du Freak</i>, which was a bit bizarre itself (but bizarre <i>unique</i>, as in a good way), all the magic, demons, zombies, portals, time travel and werewolves were too much to swallow. Still, I hated the ending of <i>The Cirque du Freak</i> series too, but I <i>have</i> reread those books more than once since the first time (although not recently), and I still remember everything that happened. With <i>The Demonata</i> I have a little trouble linking books together, unable to remember how the characters moved from one situation to another (I remember some of <i>Death’s Shadow</i> took place on a yacht, but I have no clue as to the purpose or how they got there).<br /><br /><i>Despite</i> all that, however, I’ve decided to write this review because whenever I <i>do</i> remember <i>The Demonata</i>, all I can think about are the good stuff. I can still talk about its faults – how some plot points seemed unnecessary, how the rules of magic in this universe were too undefined – but I can also talk about the great fight scenes between demons and magicians (I forgot if there was another word for who Dervish, Shark, Sharmila, and the others are – mages? People who can use magic, at any rate). I can conjure up the picture of Grubbs, Bill-E, and the other actors running away from the set of <i>Slawter</i>, pursued by Lord Loss and his familiars, clearly in my head. I can remember how the demons had stormed through a hospital, and Bec telling Dervish there were infants in the ward they were going into that needed protecting. I can taste the air of chaos that was the Demon Apocalypse, and remember how Grubbs had fought Vein and Artery in order to let Dervish finish his chess match with Lord Loss. I remember Grubbs and Kernel preparing to make one final stand against an onrush of demons after this little exchange:<br /><br />“To the death, Master Fleck?”<br />“To the death, Master Grady.”<br />“But not ours!”<br /><br />I also seem to recall that Lord Loss’ favorite chess set was one in the style of <i>The Lord of the Rings</i>.<br /><br />I can talk about the humorous side of the story as well – of Dervish, who enjoyed teasing Grubbs and Bill-E and suspected Kirilli’s father of having worked in the sewers. Of Kirilli, who was saved by one of the surviving wolves from Wolf Island from a fall, but was told by Grubbs that he only did it ‘so he could eat you later’. Of Kernel, who sarcastically reminds Beranabus that he had ‘just discovered the shining beam of light that was Grubbs Grady’ in his story. Of Timas, who in the middle of battle made up a bomb on the spot and threw it, and told Grubbs, when asked how he had done it, ‘I don’t know. I’ve never used a bomb before.’ And I can’t forget how, during his chess match with Lord Loss, Grubbs asked if he could make his pieces explode, too, in a casual ‘I couldn’t give a crap one way or the other’. These aren’t the best examples, because, as I’ve said, I can’t remember very well, and I don’t have the books with me to check, but it was always Darren Shan’s easy comedy that made me want to continue the story at least for a few more pages, and I recall not one, but several times where I would burst out laughing and have to stop to breathe for a few minutes before I could continue.<br /><br />I can also talk about the warm-heartedness of the story as well (and I’ll have to try to leave out spoilers in here). Of the love Grubbs shares with his uncle and his best friend Bill-E, of Bec’s farewell to Bran, and her talk with Dervish reminding him that she is here, alive but lonely, and Dervish’s apology to her with a hug. There are actually several sad good-byes in the series, not all of which could be dragged on for more than one sentence because of the urgency of the situation, and the hordes of merciless demons closing in…<br /><br />Most of all, though, I can talk about the characters, the refreshingly unique characters.<br /><br />Grubbs, former teenage delinquent and later savior of the world, trying to do so in as badass a way as he can manage.<br /><br />Kernel, no-nonsense apprentice to the great Beranabus, unwillingly having to work with Grubbs, able to see ‘lights’ with which he can create windows into the demon world.<br /><br />Bec, several hundred thousand years behind the times but a wonderful priestess (i.e. magician), able to absorb people’s memories by touching them, sweet and well-meaning, at least on the outside…<br /><br />Dervish, who I always imagined as the elderly Clint Eastwood, hard and callused but still ready for action, alternately serious and joking depending on what’s going on (and who he’s with).<br /><br />Beranabus, the ancient magician who had been raised in the minotaur’s labyrinth and had been a fast-running but reticent boy in Bec’s time.<br /><br />Shark, your former military soldier, complete with mohawk, tattoos on his fingers spelling ‘Shark’, and <i>several</i> little friends.<br /><br />Timas, the unfazed computer genius who in some unknown time and place had killed Shark’s family, but somehow is still on generally good terms with the fellow.<br /><br />Kirilli, the cowardly stage magician eager for attention, but only managing to be the target of most of Dervish’s (and everyone else’s) verbal insults.<br /><br />And Lord Loss, the evil-but-not-intentionally-bad demon crime boss who feeds off of humanity’s sorrow, pain and suffering, and who more than anything enjoys a good game of chess, mankind’s only worthwhile invention in his opinion.<br /><br />And many others like Sharmilla, Billy ‘Bill-E’, Juni Swan, Meera Flame, Prae Athim, and lots and lots of demons with names like Femur and Malice. It’s the characters, really, that have stayed with me the most and have made this series worth remembering. Honestly, the series is worth reading just for the characters, bad ending and all.<br /><br />Disciples. That was the word. I just checked it up; Dervish and the others are called Disciples.<br /><br />***<br /><br />2010<br /><br />I don't know, I didn't find it as believable as The Cirque du Freak series, which I loved immensely. I never liked demons, because, since there aren't so many popular rules attatched to them, you can do anything you like with them when you include them in a story. So they can look like pretty much anything you can cook up in your mind. Which turns out to be what? A half-dog half-croc woman. Get real, Shan. I found Lord Loss a little hard to picture too. Infact, if it wasn't for the help of the cover illustration for one of the later books in the series (Demon Apocalypse, I think) I probably would've had a picture of Grubbs playing against a raw piece of meat in the chess battle. <br />Moreover, the story just plain bored me. I mean, it started out okay, but it slowed down after awhile once he moves in with his uncle. Plus, I think the narrative was a little fickle-Grubbs narrative in the first chapter differs alot from how he narrates the rest of the book, like he's gone through a personality change. Hell, I liked him as a badass kid in the beginning. I mean sure, the trauma <i>would</i> do those things to a kid, but suddenly he's all chummy later on, not an angry, disturbed delinquent. It's not that I want him to be a complete bastard, but I feel like Shan isn't very good with individualizing his heroes-so far, Darren (from the Cirque du Freak series, not the author), Jebel (The Thin Executioner), and now Grubbs, they all 'sound' like the same kid. And I'm sick of it. The hellish stuff doesn't always happen to <i>just</i> the noble, compassionate kids in the literate world. Can't they have their faults? Oh, but Shan answers that one: Yeah, Grubbs has his faults-he's plain rude. Well that's enough then, isn't it?<br />Overall, I'm not interested in reading the rest of these books. My brother <i>adored</i> this one, can't see why. It wasn't in any way scary, and if there was horror, it wasn't very creative or visual. Heck, there's a <i>huge</i> absence of horror in this book. I was hurrying through some of the middle chapters just to get on with the juicy stuff. Yeah, yeah, we want everything to be gradual, let Grubbs adapt to this new world of magic and demons, but Shan could've found a way for him to do that by experiencing it instead of being told about it. We don't have any proof about magical spells except for Dervish's word on it, and well, apparently that's good enough for Grubbs. Of course when Bill-E tells him about werewolves he gets second thoughts. I'd really like to know how this kid's head works. But not so much that I wanna read the rest of these books. It's all silly anyway.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('D8xFRAsjqTIcg52YCoxqCR75Pz9y5FGNBRPbzlvbmXIHLCDBPe0yub7CGC8/apeGWqjLbJpNeOAE46vxz4VRww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oXdwPbC8tcjSd+shkwFIrvdHpfGEEBTQVm+4KmidvNKplxW4hnIuQ3A2bpam57UhsxZRomy5Pb1Xn8gV/MN0Yw==')})
  }
//]]>

  <span id="freeText4816504412064652177" style="display:none">Librarian's note: Also writes books for adults under the name <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7790731.Darren_Dash" rel="nofollow noopener">Darren Dash</a>. And in the past he has released books for adults under the names <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1304819.D_B_Shan" rel="nofollow noopener">D.B. Shan</a> and Darren O'Shaughnessy.<br /><br />Darren Shan (born July 2, 1972 in London, England) is the pen name of the Irish author Darren O'Shaughnessy, as well as the name of the protagonist of his book series The Saga of Darren Shan, also known as The Cirque Du Freak Series in the United States. He is the author of the series The Demonata, The Saga of Larten Crepsley, and Zom-B. He has also released the stand-alone novel, The Thin Executioner, and the stand-alone short novels, Koyasan, and Hagurosan. Plus, for adults, he released The City Trilogy (originally under the name of D.B. Shan), and Lady of the Shades.. </span>

        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
